                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence and genetic diversity of European ash trees"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2016 541:7636"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-12-26"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The genome sequence and genetic diversity of European ash (Fraxinus excelsior) trees reveals the species&#8217; varying susceptibility to ash dieback. Woodlands and forests around the world are increasingly susceptible to the spread of pests and pathogens resulting from climate change and global trade. In particular, ash trees across Europe and North America are currently threatened by the fungal disease ash dieback and infestation by the emerald ash borer beetle, respectively. Against this background, Richard Buggs and colleagues report the first genome sequence of an ash tree, the European ash Fraxinus excelsior, and the re-sequencing of 37 F. excelsior trees from across Europe. They find a number of genetic variants associated with reduced susceptibility to disease, and use these for an assessment of the susceptibility of host populations in an area newly under threat from the pathogen. On the basis of transcriptomic markers, they predict that ash trees in the UK will prove to be less susceptible to ash dieback than ash trees in Denmark. Ash trees (genus Fraxinus, family Oleaceae) are widespread throughout the Northern Hemisphere, but are being devastated in Europe by the fungus Hymenoscyphus fraxineus, causing ash dieback, and in North America by the herbivorous beetle Agrilus planipennis1,2. Here we sequence the genome of a low-heterozygosity Fraxinus excelsior tree from Gloucestershire, UK, annotating 38,852 protein-coding genes of which 25% appear ash specific when compared with the genomes of ten other plant species. Analyses of paralogous genes suggest a whole-genome duplication shared with olive (Olea europaea, Oleaceae). We also re-sequence 37 F. excelsior trees from Europe, finding evidence for apparent long-term decline in effective population size. Using our reference sequence, we re-analyse association transcriptomic data3, yielding improved markers for reduced susceptibility to ash dieback. Surveys of these markers in British populations suggest that reduced susceptibility to ash dieback may be more widespread in Great Britain than in Denmark. We also present evidence that susceptibility of trees to H. fraxineus is associated with their iridoid glycoside levels. This rapid, integrated, multidisciplinary research response to an emerging health threat in a non-model organism opens the way for mitigation of the epidemic."/>

data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Ian-Bancroft" data-author-popup="auth-Ian-Bancroft">Ian Bancroft</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Centre for Novel Agricultural Products, University of York" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5685.e, 0000 0004 1936 9668, Centre for Novel Agricultural Products, University of York, Heslington, YO10 5DD, York, UK" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Mario-Caccamo" data-author-popup="auth-Mario-Caccamo">Mario Caccamo</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a>,<a href="#Aff12">12</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Earlham Institute, Norwich Research Park" /><meta itemprop="address" content="Earlham Institute, Norwich Research Park, Norwich, NR4 7UZ, UK" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope"             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>Ash trees (genus <i>Fraxinus</i>, family Oleaceae) are widespread throughout the Northern Hemisphere, but are being devastated in Europe by the fungus <i>Hymenoscyphus fraxineus</i>, causing ash dieback, and in North America by the herbivorous beetle <i>Agrilus planipennis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Poland, T. M. &amp; McCullough, D. G. Emerald ash borer: invasion of the urban forest and the threat to North America’s ash resource. J. Forest. 104, 118–124 (2006)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39541e905">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Pautasso, M., Aas, G., Queloz, V. &amp; Holdenrieder, O. European ash (Fraxinus excelsior) dieback—a conservation biology challenge. Biol. Conserv. 158, 37–49 (2013)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39541e908">2</a></sup>. Here we sequence the genome of a low-heterozygosity <i>Fraxinus excelsior</i> tree from Gloucestershire, UK, annotating 38,852 protein-coding genes of which 25% appear ash specific when compared with the genomes of ten other plant species. Analyses of paralogous genes suggest a whole-genome duplication shared with olive (<i>Olea europaea</i>, Oleaceae). We also re-sequence 37 <i>F. excelsior</i> trees from Europe, finding evidence for apparent long-term decline in effective population size. Using our reference sequence, we re-analyse association transcriptomic data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Harper, A. L. et al. Molecular markers for tolerance of European ash (Fraxinus excelsior) to dieback disease identified using associative transcriptomics. Sci. Rep. 6, 19335 (2016)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39541e922">3</a></sup>, yielding improved markers for reduced susceptibility to ash dieback. Surveys of these markers in British populations suggest that reduced susceptibility to ash dieback may be more widespread in Great Britain than in Denmark. We also present evidence that susceptibility of trees to <i>H. fraxineus</i> is associated with their iridoid glycoside levels. This rapid, integrated, multidisciplinary research response to an emerging health threat in a non-model organism opens the way for mitigation of the epidemic.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>We sequenced a European ash (<i>F. excelsior</i>) tree generated from self-pollination of a woodland tree in Gloucestershire, UK. The sequenced tree (Earth Trust accession number 2451S) appeared free of ash dieback (ADB) when sampled in 2013 and 2014, but showed symptoms in February 2016. The haploid genome size was measured by flow cytometry as 877.24 ± 1.41 megabase pairs (Mbp). Total genomic DNA was sequenced to 192× coverage (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 1</a>). We assembled the genome into 89,514 nuclear scaffolds with an <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> (the length at which scaffolds include half the bases of the assembly) of 104 kilobase pairs (kbp), 26 mitochondrial scaffolds, and one plastid chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Tables 2 and 3</a>), where the non-N assembly constitutes 80.5% of the predicted genome size. RepeatMasker estimated 35.90% of the assembly to be repetitive elements, with long terminal repeat retrotransposons predominating (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Compared with other eudicot genomes of similar size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39541e978">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Ming, R. et al. Genome of the long-living sacred lotus (Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn.). Genome Biol. 14, R41 (2013)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39541e981">5</a></sup> this repeat content is low. The 17% of the assembly composed of undetermined bases probably contains additional repeats; 27% of reads that do not map to the assembly align to ash repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 5</a>). We generated approximately 160 million RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) read pairs from tree 2451S leaf tissue and from leaf, cambium, root and flower tissue of its parent tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 6</a>); low expression of repetitive elements was found in all tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><p>We annotated the genome using an evidence-based workflow incorporating protein and RNA-seq data, predicting 38,852 protein-coding genes and 50,743 transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 4</a>). This gene count is within 12% that of tomato (version of genome (v)2.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1000">4</a></sup>, potato (v3.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="et al.Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1004">6</a></sup> and hot pepper (v1.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nature Genet. 46, 270–278 (2014)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1008">7</a></sup> but higher than monkey flower (v2.0; 26,718 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hellsten, U. et al. Fine-scale variation in meiotic recombination in Mimulus inferred from population shotgun sequencing. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 19478–19482 (2013)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1012">8</a></sup>. Evidence for completeness and coherence of our models is shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>. Of 38,852 predicted genes, 97.67% (and 98.18% of transcripts) were supported by ash RNA-seq data, 81.80% showed high similarity to plant proteins (&gt;50% high-scoring segment pair coverage) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 8</a>), 97.05% had matches in the non-redundant databases (excluding hits to ash), 82.74% generated hits to InterPro signatures and 78.09% were assigned Gene Ontology terms. We also identified 107 microRNA (miRNA), 792 transfer RNA (tRNA) and 51 ribosomal RNA (rRNA) genes.</p><p>Past whole-genome duplication events are commonly inferred from the distributions of pairwise synonymous site divergence (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) within paralogous gene groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667–1678 (2004)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1030">9</a></sup>. We plotted these for ash and six other plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Ash and olive shared a peak near <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> = 0.25, suggesting an Oleaceae-specific whole-genome duplication. A peak near <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> = 0.6 shared by ash, olive, monkey flower and tomato but not by bladderwort, coffee and grape does not fit a common origin hypothesis, unless bladderwort has an accelerated substitution rate and the tomato peak is not restricted to the Solanales as evidenced previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1049">4</a></sup>. Synteny analysis between ash and monkey flower did not provide conclusive evidence for shared whole-genome duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2</a>). Duplicated genes in the ash genome that were not locally duplicated (that is, within ten genes of each other in our assembly) show no significantly enriched Gene Ontology terms at a false discovery rate level of 0.05. By contrast 1,005 locally duplicated genes showed significant enrichment of terms relating to oxidoreductase, catalytic and monooxygenase activity compared with all other genes, suggesting evolution of secondary metabolism by local duplications.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Gene sharing within and among plant genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Gene sharing within and among plant genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1597" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values between paralogous gene pairs within the genomes of ash (<i>F. excelsior</i>), tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>), coffee (<i>Coffea canephora</i>), bladderwort (<i>Utricularia gibba</i>), grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>) and monkey flower (<i>Mimulus guttatus</i>), and transcriptome of olive (<i>O. europaea</i>). <b>b</b>, Venn diagram of gene sharing by five asterid species. <b>c</b>, Venn diagram of gene sharing by six woody species. Numbers in parentheses are the total number of OrthoMCL groups found for that species; numbers in intersections show the total number of groups shared between given combinations of taxa.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM36">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM37">Source data</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We analysed gene families shared between ash and 10 other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 10</a>). In total, 279,603 proteins (77.14% of the input sequences) clustered into 27,222 groups, of which 4,292 contained sequences from all species, 3,266 were angiosperm-specific and 462 Eudicot-specific. Patterns of gene-family sharing among asterids and among woody species are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig1">Fig. 1b, c</a>. For 38,852 ash proteins, 30,802 clustered into 14,099 groups, of which 643 were ash-specific, containing 1,554 proteins. There were also 8,050 singleton proteins unique to ash. Of the 9,604 ash-specific proteins, 6,405 matched at least one InterPro signature. The 20 largest groups in ash are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Tab1">Extended Data Table 1</a>: several are putatively associated with disease resistance.</p><p>To investigate genomic diversity in <i>F. excelsior</i>, we sequenced 37 ash trees from central, northern and western Europe (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 11</a>), to an average of 8.4× genome coverage by trimmed and filtered reads. Together with reads from Danish ‘Tree35’ (<a href="http://oadb.tsl.ac.uk/">http://oadb.tsl.ac.uk/</a>), these were mapped to the reference genome. We found 12.48 million polymorphic sites with a variant of high confidence in at least one individual (quality &gt; 300 using freebayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Garrison, E. &amp; Marth, G. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907v2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   [q-bio.GN] (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1154">10</a></sup>): we refer to these as the ‘genome-wide SNP set’ in the ‘European Diversity Panel’. Of these, 6.85 million (54.88%) occur inside or within 5 kbp of genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 12</a>). We found 259,946 amino-acid substitutions and 71,513 variants that affect stop or start codons, or splice sites. We selected 23 amino-acid variants, and 26 non-coding variants from the ‘genome-wide SNP set’ with a range of call qualities for validation using KASP: individual genotype calls with quality greater than 300 have a false-positive rate of 6% and those with quality greater than 1,000 have a false-positive rate of zero (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 13</a>). We ran a more stringent variant calling restricted to regions of the genome with between 5× and 30× coverage in all 38 samples. These totalled 20.6 Mbp (2.3% of the genome), within which 529,812 variants were called with CLC Genomics Workbench. Of these, 394,885 were bi-allelic single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) with minimum allele frequency above 0.05, which we refer to as the ‘reduced SNP set’. We also found about 31,300 singleton simple sequence repeat (SSR) loci in the ash genome, and designed primers for 664 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 1</a>). In a sample of 366 of these, 48% were polymorphic in the European Diversity Panel sequences. We PCR tested 48 of these in multiplexes with European Diversity Panel genomic DNA and found that 41 amplified successfully (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome diversity of F. excelsior in Europe."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genome diversity of <i>F. excelsior</i> in Europe.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1256" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Map showing the distribution of plastid haplotypes (<i>n</i> = 37), on the basis of a median-joining plastid haplotype network for the European Diversity Panel (inset). <b>b</b>, Map showing diversity structure of genomic SNPs, on the basis of average <i>Q</i> value for each individual (inset), from three runs of STRUCTURE with different sets of 8,955 SNPs and <i>k</i> = 3. <b>c</b>, PCA of 34,607 nuclear SNPs in the European Diversity Panel, PC2 plotted against PC3, with points coloured by plastid haplotype. <b>d</b>, From the same PCA, PC1 plotted against PC2, with points coloured by groupings found by STRUCTURE using genomic SNPs. <b>e</b>, Linkage disequilibrium decay between SNPs in the European Diversity Panel. <b>f</b>, Effective population size (<i>n</i><sub>e</sub>) history estimated using the PSMC method on the reference genome, with 100 bootstraps (shown in light blue).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM38">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We analysed population structure of the European Diversity Panel using a plastid haplotype network; STRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Pritchard, J. K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945–959 (2000)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1235">11</a></sup> runs on genomic SNPs and principal component analysis (PCA) of the ‘reduced SNP set’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig2">Fig. 2a–d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 3</a>). Clearest differentiation was found in the plastid network, with four distinct haplotype groups each separated from each other by at least 20 substitutions. One group was more frequent in Great Britain than on the continental Europe. The second and third principal components of the PCA corresponded with the plastid data somewhat (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Previous analyses of SSRs in plastids identified variants unique to the British Isles and Iberia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Heuertz, M. et al. Chloroplast DNA phylogeography of European ashes, Fraxinus sp. (Oleaceae): roles of hybridization and life history traits. Mol. Ecol . 15, 2131–2140 (2006)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1248">12</a></sup>. Linkage disequilibrium in the European Diversity Panel decayed logarithmically, with an average <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> of 0.15 at 100 bp between SNPs, reaching an <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> of 0.05 at ~40 kbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig2">Fig. 2e</a>). This is similar to long-range linkage disequilibrium estimates found in <i>Populus tremuloides</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wang, J., Street, N. R., Scofield, D. G. &amp; Ingvarsson, P. K. Natural selection and recombination rate variation shape nucleotide polymorphism across the genomes of three related Populus species. Genetics 202, 1185–1200 (2016)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1266">13</a></sup>. An apparent long-term effective population size decline of <i>F. excelsior</i> in Europe was shown by analyses based on heterozygosity in the reference genome (using pairwise sequentially Markovian coalescent (PSMC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1274">14</a></sup>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig2">Fig. 2f</a>). Such patterns may also reflect a complex history of population subdivision in ash<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mazet, O., Rodríguez, W., Grusea, S., Boitard, S. &amp; Chikhi, L. On the importance of being structured: instantaneous coalescence rates and human evolution—lessons for ancestral population size inference? Heredity 116, 362–371 (2016)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1281">15</a></sup>.</p><p>We used associative transcriptomics to predict ADB damage in Great Britain. We used the full coding DNA sequence (CDS) models from our genome annotation as a mapping reference for previously generated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Harper, A. L. et al. Molecular markers for tolerance of European ash (Fraxinus excelsior) to dieback disease identified using associative transcriptomics. Sci. Rep. 6, 19335 (2016)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1288">3</a></sup> RNA-seq reads from 182 Danish ash accessions (‘Danish Scored Panel’) that have been exposed to <i>H. fraxineus</i>, and scored for damage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 2</a>). This yielded 40,133 gene expression markers (GEMs; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 3</a>) and 394,006 SNPs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 4</a>). Twenty GEMs were associated with ADB damage scores, including eight MADS-box proteins, and two cinnamoyl-CoA reductase 2 genes that may be involved in the hypersensitive response (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 5</a>). Four assays representing the top five GEMs were applied to 58 Danish accessions (‘Danish Test Panel’) to validate the top markers. Results were combined into a single predicted damage score for each tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 6</a>), which was compared with the observed damage scores (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>; <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.25, <i>P</i> = 6.9 × 10<sup>−5</sup>): predictions of damage less than 50% consistently detected trees with very low observed damage scores. The same assays were also applied to 130 accessions from across the British range of <i>F. excelsior</i> (‘British Screening Panel’; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 6</a>). Strikingly, this provided lower predictions for ADB damage in the British Screening Panel: 25% were predicted to have &lt;25% canopy damage compared with 9% of the Danish Test Panel. Trees with low predicted damage are scattered throughout Britain (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Predicted ADB damage scores in Great Britain and Denmark."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Predicted ADB damage scores in Great Britain and Denmark.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="547" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Map points are scaled by hue (high predicted damage scores in brown, low in green) and plotted according to the geographical origin of the parent trees of the British Screening Panel (<i>n</i> = 130) and the Danish Test Panel (<i>n</i> = 58). Single leaf samples taken from grafts of each individual tree were used for predicting damage scores. Inset: damage predictions for the Danish Test Panel (<i>n</i> = 58) correlated with log(mean observed damage scores) from 2013 to 2014 (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.25, <i>P</i> = 6.9 × 10<sup>−5</sup>).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM39">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM40">Source data</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also examined expression of the top five GEM loci using reads per kilobase pair per million aligned reads (RPKM) values from our shotgun Illumina read data for the reference tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 4</a>), comparing these with RPKM values from the Danish Scoring Panel. Expression patterns in the reference tree were highly correlated with those of the most susceptible Danish quartile (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.995, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.001), but not the least susceptible (<i>P</i> = 0.24), consistent with observations that the reference tree is now succumbing to the disease. We correlated the expression of all 20 top GEM markers in leaf, flower, cambium and root transcriptomes of the parent of the reference tree. This revealed that leaf expression levels were positively correlated with those in the cambium (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.65, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.001) and flower (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.38, <i>P</i> = 0.0041), but not with the root (<i>P</i> = 0.3594).</p><p>We identified putative orthologues of the five GEM loci using our OrthoMCL results (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 5</a>) and BLAST searches of GenBank, and conducted maximum likelihood and Bayesian analyses of relevant hits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 5</a>). FRAEX38873_v2_000173540.4, FRAEX38873_v2_000048340.1 and FRAEX38873_v2_000048360.1 clustered into the SVP/StMADS11 group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Smaczniak, C., Immink, R. G. H., Angenent, G. C. &amp; Kaufmann, K. Developmental and evolutionary diversity of plant MADS-domain factors: insights from recent studies. Development 139, 3081–3098 (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1427">16</a></sup> of type II MADS-box genes. FRAEX38873_v2_000261470.1 and FRAEX38873_v2_000199610.1 clustered into the SOC1/TM3 group of type II MADS-box proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Smaczniak, C., Immink, R. G. H., Angenent, G. C. &amp; Kaufmann, K. Developmental and evolutionary diversity of plant MADS-domain factors: insights from recent studies. Development 139, 3081–3098 (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1431">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wells, C. E., Vendramin, E., Jimenez Tarodo, S., Verde, I. &amp; Bielenberg, D. G. A genome-wide analysis of MADS-box genes in peach [Prunus persica (L.) Batsch]. BMC Plant Biol . 15, 41 (2015)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1434">17</a></sup>. Both groups have roles in flower development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Liu, C. et al. Direct interaction of AGL24 and SOC1 integrates flowering signals in Arabidopsis. Development 135, 1481–1491 (2008)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1438">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, D. et al. A repressor complex governs the integration of flowering signals in Arabidopsis. Dev. Cell 15, 110–120 (2008)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1441">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Dorca-Fornell, C. et al. The Arabidopsis SOC1-like genes AGL42, AGL71 and AGL72 promote flowering in the shoot apical and axillary meristems. Plant J . 67, 1006–1017 (2011)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1444">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Gregis, V., Sessa, A., Colombo, L. &amp; Kater, M. M. AGAMOUS-LIKE24 and SHORT VEGETATIVE PHASE determine floral meristem identity in Arabidopsis. Plant J . 56, 891–902 (2008)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1447">21</a></sup>, and appear to be involved in stress response in <i>Brassica rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Saha, G. et al. Genome-wide identification and characterization of MADS-box family genes related to organ development and stress resistance in Brassica rapa. BMC Genomics 16, 178 (2015)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1454">22</a></sup>. Many genes involved in regulation of flowering time in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> are involved in controlling phenology in perennial trees species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ding, J. &amp; Nilsson, O. Molecular regulation of phenology in trees—because the seasons they are a-changin’. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 29, 73–79 (2016)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1461">23</a></sup>, and genes belonging to the SVP/StMADS11 clade have potential roles in growth cessation, bud set and dormancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ding, J. &amp; Nilsson, O. Molecular regulation of phenology in trees—because the seasons they are a-changin’. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 29, 73–79 (2016)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1465">23</a></sup>. In <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>AGL22</i>/<i>SVP</i> may be required for age-related resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wilson, D. C., Carella, P., Isaacs, M. &amp; Cameron, R. K. The floral transition is not the developmental switch that confers competence for the Arabidopsis age-related resistance response to Pseudomonas syringae pv. tomato. Plant Mol. Biol. 83, 235–246 (2013)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1479">24</a></sup>.</p><p>One mechanism by which transcriptional cascades, such as those involving MADS box genes, might be involved in tolerance or resistance to pathogens is via modulation of secondary metabolite concentrations. For five high-susceptibility and five low-susceptibility Danish trees, we profiled methanol-extracted leaf samples by liquid chromatography/mass spectrometry on a quadrupole time-of-flight mass spectrometer. Partial least squares discriminant analysis (PLS-DA) clearly discriminated high- and low-susceptibility trees (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). By using accurate mass to identify the chemical nature of discriminant features, we found greater abundance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>) of iridoid glycosides (for details see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig10">Extended Data Figs 6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig11">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig12">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig13">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Data 9</a>) in genotypes with high susceptibility to ADB than in low-susceptibility genotypes. A tandem mass spectrometry (MS/MS) fragmentation network identified several product ions expected from fragmentation of iridoid glycosides (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). Iridoid glycosides are a well-known anti-herbivore defence mechanism in the Oleaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Jensen, S. R., Franzyk, H. &amp; Wallander, E. Chemotaxonomy of the Oleaceae: iridoids as taxonomic markers. Phytochemistry 60, 213–231 (2002)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1511">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kubo, I., Matsumoto, A. &amp; Takase, I. A multichemical defense mechanism of bitter olive Olea europaea (Oleaceae): is oleuropein a phytoalexin precursor? J. Chem. Ecol. 11, 251–263 (1985)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1514">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Eyles, A. et al. Comparative phloem chemistry of Manchurian (Fraxinus mandshurica) and two North American ash species (Fraxinus americana and Fraxinus pennsylvanica). J. Chem. Ecol. 33, 1430–1448 (2007)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1517">27</a></sup>. They can also enhance fungal growth <i>in vitro</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Marak, H. B., Biere, A. &amp; Van Damme, J. M. M. Systemic, genotype-specific induction of two herbivore-deterrent iridoid glycosides in Plantago lanceolata L. in response to fungal infection by Diaporthe adunca (Rob.) Niessel. J. Chem. Ecol . 28, 2429–2448 (2002)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1523">28</a></sup>, although their aglycone hydrolysis product formed following tissue damage can also mediate fungal resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Biere, A., Marak, H. B. &amp; van Damme, J. M. M. Plant chemical defense against herbivores and pathogens: generalized defense or trade-offs? Oecologia 140, 430–441 (2004)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1528">29</a></sup>. Our data suggest there may be a trade-off between ADB susceptibility and herbivore susceptibility. This is of particular concern given the threat of <i>A. planipennis</i> to ash in both North America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Poland, T. M. &amp; McCullough, D. G. Emerald ash borer: invasion of the urban forest and the threat to North America’s ash resource. J. Forest. 104, 118–124 (2006)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1535">1</a></sup> and Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Valenta, V., Moser, D., Kuttner, M., Peterseil, J. &amp; Essl, F. A high-resolution map of emerald ash borer invasion risk for southern central Europe. For. Trees Livelihoods 6, 3075–3086 (2015)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1539">30</a></sup> and may hamper efforts to breed trees with low susceptibility to both threats.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Putative iridoid glycosides as discriminatory features between F. excelsior genotypes with differential susceptibility to ADB."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Putative iridoid glycosides as discriminatory features between <i>F. excelsior</i> genotypes with differential susceptibility to ADB.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature20786/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature20786_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="663" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Multivariate analysis PLS-DA score plot of metabolic profiles of five high-susceptibility and five low-susceptibility trees (<i>n</i> = 3 per genotype). <b>b</b>, Box-plots from these profiles showing normalized (internal standard) intensity (log<sub>2</sub> transformed) of five discriminatory features observed in negative mode; <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> and retention time (RT) are given for each feature. <b>c</b>, Fragmentation network of discriminatory features, highlighted in black (positive mode) and grey (negative mode). Each product ion is labelled with its size (<i>m</i>/<i>z</i>), also depicted by its circle size. Blue shading increases with the number of times each ion is present in the precursor discriminatory features. Product ions not shared among precursors are shown as unlabelled tips. The edges are in shades of red on the basis of retention time; the paler the colour the earlier the retention time. Those fragment masses shaded in green have been previously reported from fragmentation of iridoid glycosides.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM41">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature20786/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The experiments were not randomized. The investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Tree material</h3><p>Reference tree. In 2013 twig material was collected from tree 2451S growing at Paradise Wood, Earth Trust, Oxfordshire, UK. This tree was produced via self-pollination of a hermaphroditic <i>F. excelsior</i> tree growing in woodland in Gloucestershire (latitude 52.020592, longitude −1.832804), UK, in 2002 as part of the FRAXIGEN project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="FRAXIGEN. Ash Species in Europe: Biological Characteristics and Practical Guidelines for Sustainable Use (Oxford Forestry Institute, Univ. Oxford, 2005)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1617">31</a></sup>. The parent tree was one of 19 trees that produced seed from self-pollination, and had lower heterozygosity at four microsatellite loci than the other 18 trees (D.B., unpublished observations). DNA was extracted from bud, cambial and wood tissues using CTAB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Doyle, J. J. A rapid DNA isolation procedure for small quantities of fresh leaf tissue. Phytochem. Bull . 19, 11–15 (1987)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1621">32</a></sup> and Qiagen DNeasy protocols. RNA was extracted using the Qiagen RNeasy protocol from leaf tissue of tree 2451S and from leaf, cambium, root, and flower tissue of its parent tree in Gloucestershire.</p><p>European Diversity Panel. In 2014, twig material was collected from 37 trees representing 37 European provenances in a trial of <i>F. excelsior</i> established in 2004 at Paradise Wood, Earth Trust, Oxfordshire, UK, as part of the Realizing Ash’s Potential project. DNA was extracted from cambial tissue of the twigs using a CTAB protocol.</p><p>British Screening Panel. In 2015, freshly flushed leaf material was collected from a clonal seed orchard of <i>F. excelsior</i> growing at Paradise Wood, Earth Trust, Oxfordshire, UK, for RNA extraction and complementary DNA (cDNA) synthesis as in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Harper, A. L. et al. Molecular markers for tolerance of European ash (Fraxinus excelsior) to dieback disease identified using associative transcriptomics. Sci. Rep. 6, 19335 (2016)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1636">3</a>. Single whole leaves were harvested from four ramets of each of 130 ash trees selected from phenotypically superior parents throughout Britain, which had been cloned by grafting.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">2451S DNA sequencing and genome assembly</h3><p>The genome size of 2451S was estimated by flow cytometry with propidium iodide staining of nuclei, using leaf tissue co-chopped with an internal standard using a razor blade. Three preparations were made: two with <i>Petroselinum crispum</i> ‘Curled Moss’ parsley as standard (2C genome size = 4.50 pg)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Obermayer, R., Leitch, I. J., Hanson, L. &amp; Bennett, M. D. Nuclear DNA C-values in 30 species double the familial representation in pteridophytes. Ann. Bot . 90, 209–217 (2002)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1651">33</a></sup> and one with <i>S. lycopersicum</i> ‘Stupicke polnı rane’ (2C = 1.96 pg)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Doležel, J., Greilhuber, J. &amp; Suda, J. Estimation of nuclear DNA content in plants using flow cytometry. Nature Protocols 2, 2233–2244 (2007)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1658">34</a></sup> as standard. The Partec CyStain Absolut P protocol was used (Partec, Germany). Each preparation was measured six times, with the relative fluorescence of over 5,000 particles per replicate recorded on a Partec Cyflow SL3 (Partec, Germany) flow cytometer fitted with a 100-mW green solid state laser (Cobolt Samba; Cobolt, Sweden). The resulting histograms were analysed with the Flow-Max software (version 2.4, Partec). The measurement with the tomato internal standard was used as the best estimate of genome size, because the tomato genome size is closest to that of 2451S, yielding a more accurate result.</p><p>Genomic DNA of 2451S was sequenced using the following methods: (1) HiSeq 2000 (Illumina, San Diego, California, USA) at Eurofins, Ebersberg, Germany, with 100 bp reads and shotgun libraries with fragment sizes of 200 bp, 300 bp and 500 bp, and long jumping distance libraries with 3 kbp, 8 kbp, 20 kbp and 40 kbp insert sizes, generating 188× genome coverage; (2) 454 FLX+ (Roche, Switzerland) at Eurofins with shotgun libraries and maximum read length of 1,763 bp and mean length of 642 bp giving 4.3× genome coverage; and (3) MiSeq (Illumina, San Diego, California) at the Earlham Institute, Norwich, UK, with 300 bp paired-end reads from a Nextera library with ~5 kbp insert size, giving 16× genome coverage (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 1</a>). We assembled and released five genome assembly versions over the course of 3 years, details of which can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 3</a>. The most recent version assembled first into 235,463 contigs with a total size of 663 Mbp and an <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> of 5.7 kbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 2</a>), and after scaffolding and removing organellar scaffolds, the assembly comprised 89,487 scaffolds totalling 867 Mbp (17% ‘N’) with an <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> of 104 kbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The plastid genome was assembled separately into one circular contig of 155,498 bp, including an inverted repeat region of approximately 25,700 bp. The mitochondrial genome initially assembled into 296 contigs totalling 232 kbp. After several rounds of contig extension using overlaps of mapped 454 reads, the final assembly consisted of 26 contigs totalling 581 kbp with an <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> of 60.6 kbp.</p><p>All Illumina reads from 2451S were trimmed using CLC Genomics Workbench (QIAGEN Aarhus, Denmark) versions 6–8 (depending on when the data were received) to a minimum quality score of 0.01 (equivalent to Phred quality score of 20), a minimum length of 50 bp, and were trimmed of any adaptor and repetitive telomere sequences. The MiSeq Nextera reads were also run through FLASH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Magocˇ, T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. FLASH: fast length adjustment of short reads to improve genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 27, 2957–2963 (2011)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1693">35</a></sup> to merge overlapping paired reads, and NextClip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Leggett, R. M., Clavijo, B. J., Clissold, L., Clark, M. D. &amp; Caccamo, M. NextClip: an analysis and read preparation tool for Nextera Long Mate Pair libraries. Bioinformatics 30, 566–568 (2014)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1697">36</a></sup> to remove adaptor sequences, both used with default parameters. Roche 454 reads were trimmed to a minimum Phred score of 0.05, and minimum length of 50 bp. <i>De novo</i> assembly was performed with the CLC Genomics Workbench, using the 200 bp, 300 bp, 500 bp and 5 kbp insert size Illumina library reads to build the De Bruijn graphs. The remaining Illumina reads and the 454 reads were used as ‘guidance only reads’ to help select the most supported path through the De Bruijn graphs. A word size (<i>k</i>-mer, a substring of length <i>k</i> in DNA sequence data) of 50 and maximum bubble size of 5,000 were used to assemble the reads into contigs with a minimum length of 500 bp. Contigs were then scaffolded with the stand-alone tool SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1711">37</a></sup> Basic version 2.0 using all paired Illumina reads, with the ‘-k’ parameter (number of mapped paired reads required to join contigs) set to 7. Gaps in the scaffolds were closed using the GapCloser version 1.12 program using all paired reads (except for long jumping distance libraries), with pair_num_cutoff parameter set at 7. Four hundred and fifty-four reads were mapped to the assembly and used to join overlapping scaffolds using the Jelly.py script from PBSuite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1715">38</a></sup> version 14.7.14 with the following blasr parameters: -minMatch 11 -minPctIdentity 70 -bestn 1 -nCandidates 10 -maxScore -500 -noSplitSubreads. Contig57544 was removed from the assembly because it aligned fully to the PhiX bacteriophage genome, indicating it derived from the PhiX control library added to Illumina sequencing runs.</p><p>To assemble the plastid and mitochondrial genomes, high read depth 50 bp <i>k</i>-mers were extracted from the 200, 300 and 500 bp read libraries. Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Marçais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1725">39</a></sup> version 2.1.1 was used to count the depth for each <i>k</i>-mer, and these values were plotted in a scatterplot to identify peaks that could correspond to the organellar genomes. Every <i>k</i>-mer over 600× coverage was used in a BLAST search against the NCBI non-redundant (nr) database with a filter allowing only plant sequences; <i>k</i>-mers were then extracted on the basis of whether their first hit contained a ‘mitochondrion’ or ‘plastid/chloroplast’ related description. Reads from the 200, 300 and 500 bp libraries were then filtered against the <i>k</i>-mer sets, and were kept if the first and last 50 bp matched <i>k</i>-mers from the extracted sets (reads were at most 90 bp long). Each set of reads (mitochondrial and plastid) were then assembled <i>de novo</i> using the CLC Genomics Workbench. The plastid genome assembled initially into two contigs, which were joined using an alignment to the <i>O. europaea</i> plastid genome (GenBank accession number NC_015401.1), with the inverted repeat region being identified also. Reads from the 454 library were mapped to the assembly to check the sequence and especially the join region. The mitochondrial genome assembled first into 296 contigs. To fill in gaps and join the contigs together, 454 reads were mapped against the assembly and contig ends were extended using the Extend Contigs tool in the CLC Genome Finishing Module. The Join Contigs tool was then used to join overlapping ends together, and 454 reads were mapped to the resulting assembly to check any joined regions. Using this method of ‘Map-Extend-Join’ iteratively (approximately ten times in total), a more contiguous assembly of 26 contigs was obtained.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">RNA sequencing</h3><p>The five RNA samples (see ‘Tree Material’ above) were sequenced paired-end on Illumina HiSeq 2000 with 200 bp insert sizes, and a read length of 100 bp, at the QMUL Genome Centre, London, UK. Reads were trimmed using CLC Genomics Workbench to a minimum quality score of 0.01 (equivalent to Phred score of 20) and minimum length of 50 bp, and adaptors were also removed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Analysis of repetitive DNA</h3><p>The repetitive element (transposable elements and tandem repeats) content of the ash genome was analysed via two approaches: (1) <i>de novo</i> identification of the most abundant repeat families from unassembled 454 and Illumina reads; (2) <i>de novo</i> and similarity-based identification of repeats from the ash genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7"><i>De novo</i> identification of repeat families from unassembled reads</h3><p>Individual 454 reads and Illumina read pairs from the 500 bp insert library (after adaptor trimming, but before any further quality control or filtering: see above) were used for <i>de novo</i> repeat identification. Reads were quality filtered and trimmed using the FASTX-Toolkit version 0.0.13 (<a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/index.html">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/index.html</a>). Using fastx_trimmer, the first 10 bp of all reads (454 and Illumina) were removed (owing to skewed base composition). The 454 reads were clipped to a maximum of 250 bp and Illumina reads to a maximum of 90 bp; all shorter reads were removed using a custom Perl script. Reads were then quality filtered with the fastq_quality_filter tool to retain only those where 90% of bases had a Phred score of at least 20. Exact duplicates (which are probably artefacts from the emulsion PCR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gomez-Alvarez, V., Teal, T. K. &amp; Schmidt, T. M. Systematic artifacts in metagenomes from complex microbial communities. ISME J. 3, 1314–1317 (2009)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1798">40</a></sup>) were removed from the 454 reads using the fastx_collapser tool.</p><p>The complete set of quality filtered and trimmed 454 reads (3,330,483) was used as input for the RepeatExplorer pipeline on Galaxy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Novák, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793 (2013)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1805">41</a></sup>, with a minimum of 138 bp overlap for clustering and a minimum of 100 bp overlap for assembly. All clusters containing at least 0.01% of the input reads were examined manually to identify clusters that required merging (that is, where there was evidence that a single repeat family had been split over multiple clusters). Clusters were merged if they met the following three criteria: (1) they shared a significant number of similarity hits (for example, in a pair of clusters, 10% of the reads in the smaller cluster had BLAST hits to reads in the larger cluster); (2) they were the same repeat type (for example, LINEs); (3) they could be merged in a logical position (for example, for repetitive elements containing conserved domains these domains would be joined in the correct order). The re-clustering pipeline was run with a minimum of 100 bp overlap for assembly; merged clusters were examined manually to verify that all domains were in the correct orientation.</p><p>Quality filtered and trimmed Illumina reads were paired using the FASTA interlacer tool (version 1.0.0) in RepeatExplorer, resulting in 111,230,011 pairs; unpaired reads were discarded. An initial run of RepeatExplorer with a sample of 100,000 read pairs was performed to obtain an estimate of the maximum number of reads that could be handled by the pipeline. A random sample of 3.5 million read pairs was then taken using the sequence sampling tool (version 1.0.0) in RepeatExplorer and used as input for the clustering pipeline, which further randomly subsampled the reads down to 3,370,186 pairs. The pipeline was run with a minimum of 50 bp overlap for clustering and a minimum of 36 bp overlap for assembly. Clusters containing at least 0.01% of the input reads were merged if <i>k</i><sub><i>x</i>,<i>y</i></sub> passed the 0.2 cut-off (for clusters <i>x</i> and <i>y</i>, <i>k</i><sub><i>x</i>,<i>y</i></sub> is defined as <i>k</i><sub>1,2</sub> = <i>2W</i>/(<i>n</i><sub>1</sub> + <i>n</i><sub>2</sub>) where <i>W</i> is the number of read pairs shared between clusters <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> and <i>n</i><sub><i>x</i></sub> is the number of reads in cluster <i>x</i> which does not include the other read from its pair within the same cluster); clusters that passed this threshold but which had no similarity hits to each other were not merged. The re-clustering pipeline was run with a minimum of 36 bp overlap for assembly.</p><p>Repeat families identified by RepeatExplorer were annotated according to the results of BLAST searches to the Viridiplantae RepeatMasker library, to a database of conserved protein coding domains from transposable elements and to a custom RepeatMasker library comprising all <i>Fraxinus</i> sequences (excluding shotgun sequences), all mitochondrial genome sequences from asterids and all plastid genome sequences from Oleaceae available from NCBI (downloaded on 13 February 2014); these BLAST searches were performed as part of the RepeatExplorer pipeline. For repeat families that were not annotated in RepeatExplorer (that is, no significant BLAST hits), or where only very few reads (&lt;2%) had a BLAST hit or separate reads matched different repeat types (that is, inconsistent BLAST hits), contigs were also searched against the nr/nt database in GenBank using BLASTN with an <i>E</i> value cut-off<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1880">42</a></sup> of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>, against the non-redundant database using BLASTX with an <i>E</i> value cut-off of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, and submitted to Tandem Repeat Finder version 4.07b with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res . 27, 573–580 (1999)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1892">43</a></sup>. Annotation of repeat families from the clustering of the 454 and Illumina data was cross-validated by BLAST searching the contigs from each analysis against each other using the BLASTN program in the BLAST+ package (version 2.2.28+) with an <i>E</i> value cut-off of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> and the DUST filter switched off. Any repeat families annotated as plastid or mitochondrial DNA were removed before downstream analyses (see below).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Identification of repeats from the genome assembly</h3><p><i>De novo</i> identification of repetitive elements from the assembled ash genome sequence was conducted with RepeatModeler version 1.0.7 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>) using RMBlast as the search engine. All unannotated (‘unknown’) repeat families from the RepeatModeler library were searched against a custom BLAST database of organellar genomes (see above) using BLASTN with an <i>E</i> value cutoff of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> in the BLAST+ package (version 2.2.28+ (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1922">44</a>)). Any repeat families matching plastid or mitochondrial DNA were removed.</p><p>To prevent any captured gene fragments within repetitive element families causing the masking of protein coding genes within the ash assembly, the custom repeat libraries were pre-masked using the TAIR10 CDS data set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res . 40, D1202–D1210 (2012)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1929">45</a></sup> (TAIR10_cds_20101214_updated; downloaded from <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org">http://www.arabidopsis.org</a>). First, transposonPSI version 2 (<a href="http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net">http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net</a>) was run with the ‘nuc’ option to identify any transposable-element-related genes within the TAIR10 CDS data set. Sequences with a significant hit to transposable-element-related sequences (<i>E</i> value cut-off of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) were removed from the TAIR10 CDS file (<i>n</i> = 308); a further 19 sequences that included the term ‘transposon’ in their annotation, but which did not have a hit using transposonPSI, were also removed. The filtered TAIR10 CDS data set was used to hard mask the RepeatModeler library, the RepeatExplorer libraries (454 and Illumina) and the library from RepeatMasker using RepeatMasker version 4.0.5 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) with RMblast as the search engine and the following parameter settings: -s –no_is –nolow. The four pre-masked libraries were combined into a single custom repeat library; any repeat families annotated as ‘rRNA’, ‘low-complexity’ or ‘simple’ were removed before combining the libraries. The combined library was then used to identify repetitive elements in the ash genome assembly with RepeatMasker version 4.0.5, using the same parameter settings as above. RepeatMasker results were summarized using ProcessRepeats with the species set to ‘eudicotyledons’ and using the ‘nolow’ option.</p><p>In addition to the analysis with the combined custom ash repeat library, repeats within the assembly were also annotated by running RepeatMasker separately with each of the four individual repeat libraries with parameter settings as described above. The results were saved in gff format and combined into a single gff file that was then used to inform the process of annotating protein coding genes (see below, ‘Gene annotation’).</p><p>Although the ash genome assembly covers about 99% of the expected genome size based on flow cytometry, about 17% is composed of Ns. Therefore, the repeat content of the genome assembly may be an underestimate of the actual amount of repetitive DNA within the genome. To test whether the about 18% of missing sequence includes additional repetitive elements we analysed the repeat content of individual Illumina reads that do not map to the genome assembly. Quality-trimmed and length-filtered reads from the Illumina short insert libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 1</a>) were mapped to the assembly using the ‘Map Reads to Reference’ tool in the CLC Genomics Workbench, with both similarity match and length match parameters set to 0.90. Unmapped reads from the 200 bp, 300 bp and 500 bp insert libraries (equating to about 4.8% of all reads from these libraries; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 1</a>) were searched against the custom library of ash repeats using BLASTN (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 5</a>) with an <i>E</i> value cut-off of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> and the DUST filter switched off in the BLAST+ package (version 2.2.29+ (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39541e1983">44</a>)).</p><p>To test for evidence of the expression of transposable elements, trimmed RNA sequencing reads from five different tissue types (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 7</a>) were searched against the custom library of ash repeats using BLASTN as described above for the unmapped DNA sequencing reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gene annotation</h3><p>Protein coding genes were predicted using an evidence-based annotation workflow incorporating protein, cDNA and RNA-seq alignments. Protein sequences from nine species (<i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Fraxinus pennsylvanica</i>, <i>M. guttatus</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>Pinus taeda</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature20786#MOESM43">Supplementary Table 8</a>) were soft masked for low complexity (segmasker-blast-2.2.30) and aligned to the softmasked (for repeats) final 2451S assembly with exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)" href="/articles/nature20786#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d39541e2033">46</a></sup> protein2genome version 2.2.0; alignments were filtered at a minimum 60% identity and 60% coverage, except for <i>F. pennsylvanica</i>, which were filtered at a minimum of 80% identity and 60% coverage. Publicly available <i>F. excelsior</i> expressed sequence tags (12,083 from GenBank) were aligned with GMAP (r20141229)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Wu, T.
